 550 Plays of the World Translated into Japanese in the 1920s with Stage-Photos  

    

 It is a spectacular event in the history of Japanese theater that three kinds of theatrical collection having many plays of the world translated into Japanese were published in Japan in the late 1920s.  

 At the core of the prosperous Japanese theater world around 1930 was probably the activity of the Tsukiji Little Theater Company. In support of this theater activity, three kinds of theatrical collection, all of which are to be treated here in this book, were published and sold in large numbers. Although there are duplications of translated plays in these complete works, the publication of so many world plays translated into Japanese can be said to be the marvelous sight in the world drama history. This book reproduces the great panorama of this theater world.  

 The first collection of "The Modern Drama Complete Works" (43 volumes) was published in 1928. This commemorative year marks the 100th anniversary of Ibsen's birth. This collection, which plays the most important role in this book, contains about 180 modern plays, performed almost from 1900 to 1930 and translated into Japanese by a variety of Japanese translators. There are a few things that I have noticed by browsing this modern theater collection. One is that the first volume begins in Russia. It may be natural from the fact of commemorating the 100th anniversary of Chekhov's birth. More importantly, it seems that there was a strong devotion of Japanese intellectuals, especially the theater people, to the Soviet Union after the Russian Revolution. And it can safely be said that it has determined the future of modern theater in Japan to a certain extent.  

 The second thing is that dramatists who seem to be quite minor are also actively taken up in this collection. I was educated as a college student at the department of English literature, so I can understand most of the writers from Britain, the United States and Ireland, but I cannot be convinced if it comes to dramatists from other countries. There are many playwrights who I do not know. One of the purposes of this book is to identify the original source of the translated play, but it is a much harder job than I imagined. And regrettably there are quite a few unknown dramas and playwrights in the following list.  

 In addition to the 43 volumes, one special volume was issued, which included 183 photos of modern dramas performed around 1920 especially in the Western world. From a historical point of view, this is truly a precious treasure in the world of theater. I came to think that this should not be left in the dark and I would like to make a book to preserve these photos. This book contains almost all of these photos. They are over 90 years old, and I understand that there are no copyright issues to record them here.  

 In parallel with this complete collection, another collection of 40 volumes entitled "The Complete Works of World Plays," in which about 380 plays of the world were translated into Japanese, were published from 1927 to 1931. This one is edited based on a grand idea, including not only modern plays but also classical plays such as those of Greece and Rome, and those of India and China. This again has a special volume of about 200 photos of theater scenes, each of which is very hard to obtain. Compared to the former, the condition of the photos are very good. Most of them were included in this book.  

 And the third collection of "World Drama Complete Works" was issued in 1924 in 19 volumes, which are confined only to modern plays. This had been published a few years earlier than the other two collections. So it is easy to presume that the first was based on this third collection of translated plays. I regret that this collection does not have a special volume of theatrical photos.  

 One of the objects of this book is to present to the readers very rare and precious photos of theater scenes, which are classified as three types. About 400 photos are included in the two complete works mentioned above. Those of the first collection have rough image quality, and there is a caption on the right side or bottom side of each photo, so it can easily be recognized as the original ones. On the other hand, those of the second collection have relatively good image quality, so it is difficult to distinguish them from the others I have collected, each of which has a black frame to distinguish it from the original ones.  

 The photos are accompanied with some comments in English. In principle, an English commentary of each play was basically placed under the photo. But, of the above 400 photos, there are some photos that have no translated plays in the entire collection. In these cases, an English commentary on the picture is paced above the photo.  

 Last of all, the following points should be noted on the writing of each work.  

 ãƒ» The year after the work intends to list the year when the play was published, but it is not clear that every year is so due to lack of data.  

 ãƒ» The name of a play is shown in the original language. Also, I tried best to write every title also in English as much as possible.  

 ãƒ» For something unclear or ambiguous about the names of the play or the playwright, I put the question mark (?).  

 ãƒ» Each play translated into Japanese is indicated in the following order.

 ã€Œ Japanese titl eã€ ( Ile  1917) Playwright's Name in Japanese (Eugene O'Neill 1888 - 1953) Japanese Translator

 [A scene from  The Seagull  ( Ğ§Ğ°Ğ¹ĞºĞ°  1895) written by Anton Chekhov and directed by Constantin Stanislavski (Russian theatre practitioner, 1863 - 1938) and Vladimir Nemirovich-Danchenko at the Moscow Art Theater on December 29, 1898. The cast included Stanislavski as Trigorin and Vsevolod Meyerhold as Konstantin, Olga Knipper as Arkadnia, Maria Lilina as Masha, Maria Roksanova as Nina, Yevgeniya Raevskaya as Polina, Ioasaf Tikhomirov as Medvedenko, Vasily Luzhsky as Sorin, Alexander Vishnevsky as Dorn, and Alexander Artem as Shamrayev. This was the first production in Moscow, though the play had been performed with only moderate success in St. Petersburg two years earlier.]
